```

"Kellie Fitton" <KELLIEFITTON@YAHOO.COM> wrote in message
news:1114843913.456027.74340@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com...
> Hi Pete,
>
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I think that has definite possibilities. I'd like to see it in action before
deciding, but the idea is imaginative and useful.

> 2). Inflated 3D control --- this is a programming technique were you
> create
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Another good idea.

> 3). Help toolBar --- this is a context help toolBar that can be
> activated
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

This is akin to "Query by Example", another useful technique. Sometimes
showing somebody an example of something beats the pants off trying to
explain it, within the limited confines of an error message.

I note in passing that none of the above have any impact on user key strokes
or clicks (for those here who really care about that... We should bear in
mind that the number of strokes/clicks in a pure data entry, high volume
input application, will certainly be MUCH more important than in the case of
someone entering data onto a form that is the front end for an application.
The difference between 100,000 keystrokes and 90,000 is important; the
difference between 18 and 20 may still be important but certainly not as
critical.)

>
> BottomLine pete,
> when my COBOL ship arrives, I am gonna drop it like its Hot. ;---))

Don't quite understand this last, but you have certainly demonstrated that
you have no problem with solving problems.

The steps you went through are excellent.

1. You  asked for input. (In CLC and amongst your peers.)
2. You assiuduously separated the wheat from the chaff, using your own
reasoning and requirements.
3. You followed up on the good advice (I was pleased to see you followed
Bill's links, as one example of this.)
4. You thought about what you discovered from these sources and applied
imagination to them.
5. You were not deterred when your ideas received some ice water.
6. You did not accept the 'argument from authority'. People who may be more
knowledgeable and experienced than you are, are not infallible (I include
myself in this... :-)). You correctly decided that arguments stand on the
merit or otherwise of the argument, and not on the 'authority' of the person
offering the argument. (Having said that, it is fair to expect that people
with some expertise on a subject will make 'better' arguments than people
who are making it up as they go along...but ALL arguments made by ANYBODY
must stand on their own merit finally. [Damn, I just realised that is a
sweeping statement and therefore cannot be valid... never mind, draw your
own conclusions... :-)])

I believe that when you move into the real world, the approaches you are
developing now will stand you in good stead.

I further believe that your applications will be considered user friendly,
and that is what you were trying to achieve.

Remember that the worst that can happen with a new approach, is that it is
found not to work well in practise. You must go back to users 3 months later
and get all feedback, honestly and without prejudice. Explain to them that
you are looking for ways to improve the system, it is 'safe' for them to
criticize, and don't get angry if they come up with things you think are
trivial.

Find out what they really like, and what they really don't, then amend
accordingly. I call this 'interactive iteration'. It means your system will
'evolve' towards a better result than if it is just designed then
implemented.

You are well on the right track to being an effective problem solver.

Pete.
```

```
Hi Richard, LX-i,

In a graphical environment application (a dialogBox), when an editBox
control
should contains the current-date as the default date as you have
mentioned, then
the programmer would simply initializes the editBOX with the current
date before
plopping the dialogBox on the screen --- then, if the end-user chooses
to modify
that date, then he or she simply select the new desired date from the
visual
calendar (Date-Time-Picker), and overRides the default date in the
editBOX
accordingly.

Moreover, if an editBOX is designed for a date-field for which there is
not yet a date,
such as "completion date", then again, the programmer can initializes
this field with
zeros before showing the dialogBox on the screen, and if the end-user
needs to
change that date, a simple click on the visual calendar will allow the
new date to
replace the zeros in the editBOX accordingly. Therefore, leaving the
Date Field
blank is really useLess and does not provide any major advantage
whatsoever.

Again, I am not trying to force anyOne to use the visual calendar,
however, allow
me to accentuate the benefits that you can reap from using the
Dat-Time-Picker
control window, which you canNot acquire by using the keyBoard or the
cut/paste
approach. Open the flood gate:

1). End-user canNot paste illegal characters in the dateField.

2). End-user canNot enter an invalid date format.

3). End-user canNot enter an inaccurate date (min & max).

Ok Richard, flareOn and set it onFire. :---))

Regards, Kellie.
```

```
> Again, I am not trying to force anyOne to use the visual calendar,

Though it seems that you not offering your users anything else.

> accentuate the benefits that you can reap from using the
Dat-Time-Picker
> control window, which you canNot acquire by using the keyBoard or the
> cut/paste approach. Open the flood gate:

I have absolutely no doubt about the possible advantages of a
date/picker, which is why I have one available for any users who _want_
to use it.

> 1). End-user canNot paste illegal characters in the dateField.

Actually, it is not hard to enforce that with some quite simple code.

> 2). End-user canNot enter an invalid date format.

Actually, it is not hard to enforce that with some quite simple code,
or perhaps even a control. When I was writing stuff for Windows (in
Cobol) I often used FarPoint toolbox for the date entry (and for other
controls) which allowed the user to enter dates by keyboard (or paste)
or use the spin control to increment/decrement, _or_ select from a
popup calendar.

> 3). End-user canNot enter an inaccurate date (min & max).

CALL WinAPI "DateSetDlgItemRange" using ....

You should just get over your idea that there is only one way to
achieve your claims.

> Ok Richard, flareOn and set it onFire. :---))

You're not wrong about the date picker, you are only wrong in
dismissing everything else and claiming that your way is the only way.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Are you as polite and diplomatic as COBOL?...

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Kellie Fitton" <KELLIEFITTON@YAHOO.COM>
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T15:13:05-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1114985585.890914.90350@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1114846562.992359.281840@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1114714963.943680.313040@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <joe_zitzelberger-EF2015.08431029042005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <1114786309.549006.320860@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <Lfwce.1165208$8l.136470@pd7tw1no> <1114813666.325616.188440@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <1114830605.374732.15410@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com> <1114833616.654151.326280@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com> <1114837108.809416.308770@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <1114844935.493058.184070@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <1114846562.992359.281840@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi Richard,

you have been my COBOL mentor for quite someTime on C.L.C. group, but
so far you
have NOT contributed any ideas or thoughts about the PIVOTAL point and
concerns
when the end-user makes a Mistake.

here is two cute error messages I got from a friend of mine regarding
the end-user's
mistakes when using a dialogBox:

1). An error message if the end-user clicks the "Save" button without
entering the
required data in all the editBox fields:  "My god... its you again.".

2). An error message if the end-user clicks the "Save" button after
receiving a Prior error
message notifying him/her about the gravity of their first mistake:

"Unbelievable... you still there?."  ;---))

Now, if you stopped laughing you can readOn:

let me explain why you are mixing apples with oranges, one more time.
:---))
if you noticed in my first posting the remarks I have PointedOut about
a messageBoX as
a solution to alert the end-user, I Clearly said that this option will
INTERRUPT the
process of the program, simply because, by default, the messageBox will
be displayed
on the screen and will NOT go away, until the end-user hits the enter
key or click on the
provided button.

That said, here is the pivotal Point of my posting and concerns that
should be deduced
from the implied logic: How to alert the end-user about their Mistakes
quietly, gently,
politely, diplomatically and without interrupting the flow of the
program execution, so
he/she can correct their mistakes or provide the missing data
Uninterrupted.

On the other hand, if my dialogBox requires dataEntry for DATES or
TIMES, I can simply
display the Date-Time-Picker visual calendar, and solicit the needed
information
accordingly, since their is NO error message have been displayed on the
screen, and the
flow of the program execution is peachy keen.

"Consistency is the hobgoblin of tiny minds." 

Regards, Kellie.
```

```
> but so far you have NOT contributed any ideas or thoughts about
> the PIVOTAL point and concerns when the end-user makes a
> Mistake.

Since when was that compulsory ?

I respond to the bits that _I_ want and reject any notion that there
are prerequisites for doing so.

> I have PointedOut about a messageBoX as a solution to alert
> the end-user,

My message was simply correcting your mistaken view that the user was
_forced_ to click. I pointed out that there was no need to use the
mouse, which was a significant issue with user interfacing.

> I Clearly said that this option will INTERRUPT the process of the
> program, simply because, by default, the messageBox will
> be displayed on the screen and will NOT go away, until the
> end-user hits the enter key

And that may be a _good_ thing.

Let us see what may happen if the user is not prevented from continuing
to key, say there is a customer number input:

* User keys custno but transposes two characters, hits tab and
continues typing next field data.

* program checks code puts error message next to custno and sets focus
back to custno.

* keystrokes targeted at next data field corrupts custno.

Alternately:

* User keys custno ...

* program puts up dialog with error.

* keystokes are discarded until user notices there is an error.  Custno
preserved and allows editing to remove transposition.

Now, I am not advocating one or the other method, but merely indicating
that your dismissal of the method may be inappropriate.

And what you _completely_ fail to notice is that the process _needs_ to
be interrupted and the user needs to correct the data item before
continuing.

> or click on the provided button.

Well, actually you _only_ considered click, which was the point of my
message in the first place, one you failed to notice.

Also if you failed to notice that this was a contribution then you were
probably 'using your mouth when you should be using your ears', as the
saying goes.

> here is two cute error messages I got from  ....

You appear to be discarding the whole concept of stop points simply
because some put rude messages on them.

> without interrupting the flow of the program execution,

It has nothing to do with 'program execution', which is done with
callbacks (interrupts) anyway, it is about the flow of user input.

> so he/she can correct their mistakes or provide the missing data
> Uninterrupted.

Unfortunately you seem ignore users who are fast data entry and will
already have keyed the next field before they notice that there is an
error.  If they have to visually check the result of a field entry
before continuing to the next they will be slowed down, if the keys
they have typed corrupt the error field they will be slowed down. One
way to provide for setting back the focus when an error is detected is
to discard keystrokes until they acknowledge the error.  This protects
the data they have already entered into that field.

> On the other hand, if my dialogBox requires dataEntry for DATES
> or TIMES, I can simply display the Date-Time-Picker visual
> calendar, and solicit the needed information accordingly,

Potentially _FORCING_ the user to switch to mouse and slowing them
down.  While a date picker is a good thing it should usually be just
one _option_ and available when the _user_ wants it not as the only way
to enter a date. Actually my date picker calendar works with just a
keyboard too.

For example I usually set a date field to some default and then allow
it to be keyed over.  if they _request_ a calendar picker it starts
with the date entered. So if the field expects yymmdd and they put in
0412 and hit F8 (or click the calendar button) the get the calendar for
december 2004.

> "Consistency is the hobgoblin of tiny minds."

What you also failed to notice was that I was pointing out your
_inconsistency_ and then criticisng _consistency_. Do try and keep up.
```

```
jce wrote:
> 
> Having tried to book flights online in the last few weeks, the "date picking 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Having mentioned Quickbooks - let me mention some other features. 
Firstly there is only ONE file per corporation, no transaction files 
etc... I run it WITHOUT Chart-of-Account numbers - you can just use 
descriptions. Died in the wool CAs just *hate* that feature - although 
it provides an exhaustive audit trail.

As indicated the Date-Picker pops up everywhere. There is one Intuit 
feature that I find irritating. You can automatically bring up Balance 
Sheet, P & L, Trial Balance etc for any period of time - so there are 
two date fields 'Beginning' and 'Ending', (only the 'Ending Date' for 
the Balance Sheet). They are respectively in the format "mm/dd/yy" 
including the obliques - AND - want to change them, they are THREE 
separate fields "mm", "dd" "yy". Want to change them with 'Insert" - 
forget it - you have to backspace on each, then enter the fresh element. 
There's a case where the Date-Picker most effectively comes into play. 
And of course a report is hardly in the category of data entry.

"Writing cheques" as opposed to actually "Printing Cheques", (I used it 
as a convenience, but this year went with a Journal Entry - much quicker 
and contains my cheque references). As indicated on screen a dummy 
cheque and a G/L Distribution portion. As default brings up current date 
for the first cheque. Now use Date-Picker to select date you actually 
want. It stays with the selected date until you change it. Now you can 
either use the Date-Picker again, or this one which I like -  click on 
the cheque's displayed date and use the Numpasd + or - to 
increment/decrement the date - there's a neat feature for PECD and his 
Component.

Back to the Chart of Accounts and other descriptive files (which I don't 
use), Vendors/Suppliers, Customers etc. You can pop-up the C of A as a 
separate Dialog which we would call a Listbox. Accounts listed 
traditionally, Balance Sheet, Assets, Liabilities, then Expenses etc. 
Plus the current balance for each. You can Mouse click to select an 
account, obviously.

But when writing cheques etc., as soon as I go to enter, say "Revenue 
Canada" it brings up the first description which matches what you have 
keyed so far - OK so one has to bear in mind your descriptions when 
first setting up the appropriate "List".

I'm not sure - but I think the approach is close to PECD's 'soundex'.
Somebody asked on the M/F Forum about 'Searching' and got the reply "Go 
looking for Activex etc.". Already having a considerable number of my 
own OO support classes available, intrigued, I thought I'd give it a 
shot. (Ironically one of the early M/F OO examples contains such a 
search technique - but new to OO and GUIs - I had ignored it, with no 
comprehension of what was going on).

Basically the only addition I had to make was produce a Search Dialog as 
an an instance of my Dialog template to contain a Query field and a 
Listbox containing a Sorted Collection, ascending alpha by description. 
Within the template I have method "wantAllKeys" which captures all 
keystrokes. So back to Revenue Canada.

As soon as you key in "R" it goes looking, and firms up say, when you 
have "Rev" and highlights the entry in the Listbox. The only other 
feature required - make the text for 'query' and the Listbox entry the 
same case - so I make them both UPPERCASE for the search comparison. 
Naturally if you had a HUGE list then do a binary chop to locate you 
nearer to the entries in the Listbox that are relevant to the current 
search.

Using the "Rev" :-

if inputText(1:n) = ListboxText(1:n) then highlight in the Listbox

Not quite the match the user was after, they key in some further 
characters until they match - then user clicks on the 'Selected'.

Like all things in this game, once you have it figured, it becomes dead 
easy.

Jimmy
```

```
Hi JCE,

You are missing the main point --- the Date-Time-Picker does NOT fall
into the annoying
category at all, the purpose of the visual calendar is to eliminate the
needs for typing
a date or time, or perhaps to limit the error typos that the end-user
can accidentally
insert into the editBox. However, after you click the desired date from
the visual calendar,
it is chiefly the programmer's responsibility to normalize the selected
date and display it
in an easy-to-read language, case in point: April 5, 2005.

Typing or PASTING your own desired dates or time in an entryBox, canNot
be construed
as quick or convenient way of dataEntry, especially when the accuracy
of the Input is the
main pivotal point, also, does NOT guarantee what you had typed is
Correct or even have
a Valid Format, not to mention, you could even leave the date field
completely blank,
which will force the programmer to write MORE code just to ensure the
validity of the
date format, and this approach is considered inefficient, obsolete and
wasteful, not to
mention it is so 1988, a year where the Keyboard ruled and felt like a
happy fat cat,
simply because we did not have a cute mouse around.

bottomLine, sophisticated whizz bang cobol programmers...

Regards, Kellie.
```

```
Richard wrote:
>>Typing or PASTING your own desired dates or time in an entryBox,
> 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> does not make it some sort of absolute truth.

I agree.

>>you could even leave the date field completely blank,
> 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> example, today.

The latter is what I've done on several occasions, and it really bugs me 
to have to enter today's date on something that should know better.  :)

In my golf tournament software, the registration screen allows the date 
to be blank, which means they registered "today".  In my prayer request 
tracking software, the entry screen has a date, but leaving it blank 
means "today" - the modification screen has a check-box instead of a 
date, and if you check it, the "updated" date isn't changed (for typo 
corrections, changing "this past week" to "last week", etc.).

At work, most screens with dates on them have to do with something that 
has happenED, so dates are required to be entered, and usually have to 
fall within a certain range.

>>which will force the programmer to write MORE code just to ensure the
> 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> The convenience should be for the _user_, not the programmer.

This reminds me of how I learned to validate a date using Javascript. 
The syntax was something like this (and my memory is not quite as fresh 
as it should)...

if (!parseInt(Date.parse([inputField].value))) {
     alert("Not a date!");
     [inputField].focus();
     return(false);
}

That parse method would take pretty much anything, and if it could 
convert it to a date, you were good to go.  You could then use the value 
of that parseInt method to format the date in any way you wanted.  While 
COBOL doesn't have a parseInt or Date.parse, I'm sure in the PC world 
there are ways to mimic that behavior.
```

```
Well, you don't really want them saying what they're *going* to do to 
the plane to fix it, you want to know what they actually *did*, which 
means after-the-fact recording.  :)  What happens a lot of the time is 
that when the maintenance actions occur, the mechanic writes down what 
he did.  Then, if there's someone in the flight with a hurt leg, or 
something else that keeps them off the flight line, they get to be the 
ones who actually put the information into the system.

(To be fair, and somewhat back on-topic, the screen where you enter job 
data *does not* assume your user ID if it is blank - it makes you enter 
one anyway, even if it's your own.  Of course, the screen is probably 
close to 30 years old at this point...)

> spoken to Programmers about tails and dogs, but it sometimes falls on deaf
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> mile is educational and satisfying.

That's definitely a good concept to understand.  In the Air Force's 
programmer training, along the lines of "efficiency", they teach that 
the system should be "as efficient as required."  They go on to explain 
that there is a balance between efficiency, usability, and 
maintainability - and striking a balance favoring the latter two *may* 
mean slight compromises in the former.
```

```
> a big Fan of using TouchScreen monitors

I hate people leaving fingerprints on my monitors, or even on their
monitors if I have to use them.

> that shows how to use this programming concept with COBOL language,

In the late 70s Pizza Hut in the UK (and maybe elsewhere) put in
touchpad systems from ICL (for whom I worked).  Many restaurants seem
to still use these devices though they are more recent.

The ICL touchpad systems were programmed in:  ... Cobol.  That is the
terminals had Z80 CPUs and ran CP/M or a clone of this plus a version
of MS Cobol 1.x.
```

```
> Is it true that the only trick to activate
> a touchScreen program, is in the special
> screen DRIVERS that comes with the
> monitor, and NOT the actual COBOL
> source code per se ??

Having never programmed a touchscreen (though I did work on the ICL
touchpanel a quarter century ago), and especially not a recent one, I
can only say that it is quite likely different for each different
device.

Some may simply emulate a mouse, others may have a different API, yet
others may do it entirely differently.

I do recall with the MS Cobol that it was all done with calls to
library routines in the run-time and used array of bits to turn the
touchpanel lights on or off or flashing and made the touch positions
enabled or disabled.
```

```

"Kellie Fitton" <KELLIEFITTON@YAHOO.COM> wrote in message
news:1115078438.186242.201770@f14g2000cwb.googlegroups.com...
> Hi Pete,
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Richard was right in his comment that it varies.

My experience is that the driver does the work and returns the x, y,
co-ordinates, so you can access them if you want to, just as the mouse
driver can for a mouse move event. Fields on the screen gain and lose focus
when touched, exactly as they would if clicked by a mouse. A click event is
raised if the user taps the screen (in the right place, of course).

It is an event driven model and I used OO COBOL in the one I did. Looking at
the COBOL code you could not detect whether it was a touch screen device or
a normal mouse.

Nothing magical or mystical.... :-)

Pete.
```

```
"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message
news:1176rr9a2gnhc6@corp.supernews.com...

> In other words, both seem to have unnecessary
> and incorrect capitalization.

It was common in formal written English of this era to capitalize nouns,
just as it is mandatory today in written German.  Because this style does
not match 21st-century conventions does not necessarily mean that it was
either "incorrect" or "unnecessary" at the time it was written.   I do not
believe it is appropriate to convey any particular Distinction between
capitalized Words and uncapitalized Words in written English of this Era.
We preserve some of this convention in our use of capital letters in titles.

To presume that some semantic weight should be given to a distinction
between capitalized and uncapitalized nouns in formal written English of
this period is as dangerous to proper interpretation as presuming that
"thou" and "you" are interchangeable when reading the King James Version of
the Bible.  The translators thereof were *rigorously* preserving a
distinction present in the original texts that has, alas, been lost to
modern English (and reintroduced in a different form in some dialects of
American Southern English).

    -Chuck Stevens
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<117dfdhsis7lhb3@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<1e50e.2183556$Zm5.353953@news.easynews.com> <2e2e9$42682377$45491db9$25741@KNOLOGY.NET> <d49gj5$48o$1@panix5.panix.com> <76ccf$42685510$45491db9$15115@KNOLOGY.NET> <d4afih$e3$1@panix5.panix.com> <3910e$426d924e$45491c57$29869@KNOLOGY.NET> <d4lg2e$phv$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <d1fcd$426edb81$45491c57$12550@KNOLOGY.NET> <d4o1it$nfq$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <d4oca3$2jie$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <b3ba2$42704adf$45491c57$12482@KNOLOGY.NET> <1170ktbsni2tl56@corp.supernews.com> <b07d7$427182e5$45491c57$18112@KNOLOGY.NET> <11739asa5l09p80@corp.supernews.com> <3dc2b$4272fa8b$45491c57$11686@KNOLOGY.NET> <1176rr9a2gnhc6@corp.supernews.com> <d55jo7$1gj0$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```

"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote in message
news:d55jo7$1gj0$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com...
> "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message
> news:1176rr9a2gnhc6@corp.supernews.com...
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> We preserve some of this convention in our use of capital letters in
titles.

Let me acknowledge that I should have used 'or'
instead of 'and'. I used 'seem' to suggest the
appearance of error by the conventions of today,
as I understand them to be, rather than a claim of
error in absolute terms.

I found your use of 'this', to refer to the 18th-century,
to be interesting, since 'this' conventionally refers to
the near and 'that' to the remote, and the 18th-century
seems, to me, to be the remote.

> To presume that some semantic weight should be given to a distinction
> between capitalized and uncapitalized nouns in formal written English of
> this period is as dangerous to proper interpretation as presuming that
> "thou" and "you" are interchangeable when reading the King James Version
of
> the Bible.  The translators thereof were *rigorously* preserving a
> distinction present in the original texts that has, alas, been lost to
> modern English (and reintroduced in a different form in some dialects of
> American Southern English).

I agree, though it did not cross my mind to give any
weight to capitalization; instead I relied on context.
Given the context, "... [Men] ... their Creator ...", I
believe this to be equivalent to "...  [each man] ... his
creator ..." and, therefore, 'creator' is an abstraction
(the act of considering something as a general quality
or characteristic apart from any concrete realities,
specific object, or actual instance) or, perhaps, a
metaphor (the application of a word or phase to an
object or concept it does not literally denote, in order
to suggest comparison with another object or concept),
and should not be capitalized by today's conventions.
Had it been written "the Creator" or "our Creator", the
capitalization would be proper by today's conventions,
since any equivalence would be denied.

I did not rely solely on logical analysis. I was aware of
a connection between Rousseau's, "The Social Contract,"
and the founding of the United States, particularly in the
context of separation of church and state.

Rousseau's, "The Social Contract," Book IV, Chapter 8
< http://www.constitution.org/jjr/socon_04.htm#008 >.

"The subjects then owe the Sovereign an account of
their opinions only to such an extent as they matter to
the community. Now, it matters very much to the
community that each citizen should have a religion.
That will make him love his duty; but the dogmas of
that religion concern the State and its members only so
far as they have reference to morality and to the duties
which he who professes them is bound to do to others.
Each man may have, over and above, what opinions
he pleases, without it being the Sovereign's business to
take cognisance of them; for, as the Sovereign has no
authority in the other world, whatever the lot of its
subjects may be in the life to come, that is not its
business, provided they are good citizens in this life."

All of Rousseau's, "The Social Contract," is available
at < http://www.constitution.org/jjr/socon.htm >.
```

```
"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message
news:117dfdhsis7lhb3@corp.supernews.com...
>
> "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote in message
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> > just as it is mandatory today in written German.  Because this style
does
> > not match 21st-century conventions does not necessarily mean that it was
> > either "incorrect" or "unnecessary" at the time it was written.   I do
not
> > believe it is appropriate to convey any particular Distinction between
> > capitalized Words and uncapitalized Words in written English of this
Era.
> > We preserve some of this convention in our use of capital letters in
> titles.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> seems, to me, to be the remote.

Point taken.  I was using "this" to refer to "the era under discussion"
(namely, the late 18th century); the "topic at hand", if you will.


> > To presume that some semantic weight should be given to a distinction
> > between capitalized and uncapitalized nouns in formal written English of
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> since any equivalence would be denied.

The question I have is whether the average writer of formal 18th century
English would have differentiated between "their creator" and "their
Creator", or for that matter even "our creator" and "our Creator"; it seemed
to me that was at least part of the underlying presumption.

In the same sentence in which "their Creator" appears in the Declaration of
Independence, so also do "Rights", "Life", "Liberty", "Happiness", and in
the next sentence "Governments", "Men", and so forth.  Point being,
"Creator" mid-sentence in *modern* written English would ordinarily be taken
synonymous with "God" on the grounds of the conventional capitalization of
names for the Deity.  That presumption, if it was appropriate at all in that
dialect of English, would be quite a bit less likely.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"""shiva the creator of dance

# Shiva - The God Of Destruction. Part 3
... , he is known as Neelkantha, the blue-necked one. NATARAJA - THE
LORD OF DANCE Shiva is the creator of dance and of the first 16
rhythmic syllables ever uttered, from which the Sanskrit ...
""""

""" ---------------
Shiva Nataraja is considered to be the god of dance in Hindu mythology.
His dancing image, in the Tandava form, is the starting point of all
creation. To the dancer the four arms of the Nataraja are a depiction
of dance movement in an immovable and static medium. The mystique of
the arms and legs of the figure has a cosmological significance as the
dance is taken as merely a human representation of a cosmic fact. In
the Nataraja image the frontal palm of the right hand which is lifted
and slightly bent represents security (abhaya) to devotees. The left
hand which is thrown across the body with the fingers pointing
downwards indicates the feet of the Lord as the refuge of devotees. The
upraised left foot represents the blessing bestowed by the Lord. In the
right upper hand Shiva carries a small drum representing the creative
sound which began the universe, and in the other hand he has a fire
which is symbolic of light and therefore destruction of ignorance.
Under the right foot is a dwarf, which signifies triumph over evil.
Encapsulated in this figure of the Dancing Lord is the entire function
of Shiva as the creator, preserver and destroyer. This dance is a
metaphor for the belief that life is essentially a dynamic balancing of
good and bad, where opposites are interdependent. The dance of Shiva is
the dance of life.
"""

Of course you are entitled to have whichever 'creator' you prefer in
your own sect.

There was a recent BBC documentary in 'The Colour of War' series where
archives of colour film have been put together to destroy the myth that
between 1900 and 1950 the world was black and white.  This was
following Hitler and was overdubbed by contempory writing and letters.
One had it that Hitler was the creator of the universe, such is the
nature of theism. I am sure that one could easily find believers who
think that Idi Amin, or Kim Il-sung is the 'creator of the universe and
giver of all life', just as they did about Jehovah when he was some
local warlord.
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> On 29-Apr-2005, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:

He was referring to the people he could see jumping or falling out of
the collapsing wreck.  There was a documentary about this recently;
several survivors were interviewed and with them, the bit of the film
that showed them getting out - very dramatic but terrifying footage. 
Incidentally, another documentary concludes pretty convincingly that the
Hindenburg burned up so quickly because its skin material contained
powdered aluminium - Shuttle fuel!  Pieces of the skin still exist so
that part of the analysis is absolutely solid.  The rest of it is due to
static electricity in the air and the lack of grounding of the various
skin peices - a bit difficult to convey as I didn't fully follow it.

PL
```

```

On  2-May-2005, Peter Lacey <lacey@mb.sympatico.ca> wrote:

> > It is interesting how the Hindenberg disaster got someone upset that he
> > created
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> the collapsing wreck.

It still seems odd.   If he wasn't under such emotional stress, I expect he
probably would have edited that to "oh the people".    We don't see humanity
jumping or falling out of a dirigible, we see people.

At any rate - quotes made from his broadcast aren't referring to people as such
- they refer to disaster.   "Oh the Humanity" means "What a Calamity" or "How
Terrible", not "Oh the people".

There's another word that I can place its origin.   In the U.S. "tarmac" is used
exclusively to mean "airport apron" - and our airport aprons are usually made
out of concrete.    USAmericans didn't use the term "tar Macadam" to mean
asphalt, and we were unfamiliar with the phrase until the BBC reported about the
hostages at the airport at Entebbe.    He mentioned the people lined up on the
tarmac, USAmericans saw that they were lined up on the airport apron, and we
inferred wrongly what tarmac meant.
```

```
LX-i wrote:

> Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
…[64 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Read some of the Anne Perry novels or take a look at the history of most 
western countries.  It was assumed that the nobility had mistresses. 
The red-light districts were in most communities of any size.  While 
things were a lot more hidden in the 1950's when I was in high school 
the following things happened at my middle class suburban high school.
1.  At least one thirteen year old was reported to have to get married.
2.  One guy in my gym class described being scared when his condom broke 
and that changed to practicing withdrawal after that.  He also claimed 
to know every inch of his girl friend's body.
3.  When I was a senior there was a rumor that twin freshman girls were 
performing oral sex.
4.  I heard later that one of the cheerleaders in my class had group sex 
with a number of members of the football team.
5.  The double standard was alive and well.

While blatant sexual messages are far more prevalent now in the US and 
Canada provided by both the entertainment and advertising communities, 
the 1950's were no era of idyllic innocence and from some of the things 
my mother said about things she had heard or read in the newspapers when 
she was young in the 1910 - 1920 era, it wasn't that innocent and 
pristine then.

I frankly think that from a Christian point of view we should applaud 
the change that children whose parents aren't wed to each other or whose 
parents weren't married at time of conception are not longer shunned or 
looked down upon.  That doesn't mean that I am for anything goes but 
that we don't shun people for that which they have no control over.

We do need to think through what societal curbs are needed on sexual 
activity from the points of view of public health, community order, and 
child protection.  The past prohibitions had their societal purpose as 
did the bending of the rules.  It is interesting to note that most 
societies and religions are not thrilled with giving free reign to our 
"natural" desires and try to bound them.  The repression attempts all 
too often went overboard but I have the sense that current day European 
and North American society goes too far the other way.  I know that 
various Islamic societies would agree with that assessment.  On the 
other hand many of the customs and implementations of restrictions in 
many Islamic societies violate my conception of human right and from 
some Islamic commentators I have heard also violate the spirit and 
teachings of the Koran.

If the preceding comments seem somewhat unclear and contradictory, that 
is because my views and beliefs are currently in a state of flux.
```

```
I don't think you yet understand my concern.

You wrote "We don't tell European and Mexican immigrants that they have to
renounce Catholicism, because we're a Protestant nation."

According to my understanding of English grammar (including the use of the
comma), the difference between this statement and "Because we're a
Protestant nation we don't tell European and Mexican immigrants that they
have to renounce Catholicism."

It appears to me the *assertion* is "We are a Protestant nation", and the
*conclusion* to be drawn from that assertion is "We don't tell ...
immigrants ...".

Why is the assertion true?   The wording in Article 11 of the Treaty of
Tripoli (1797) would argue *strongly* against it, and as to the assertion
that it was obviously an international ruse it should be pointed out that
the U. S. Senate *unanimously* approved it just eight years after the
ratification of the constitution, and John Adams, arguably a Founding
Father, "proudly" proclaimed it to the nation.

How does that *conclusion* follow from the stated *assertion*, presuming the
assertion has been demonstrated.

The two seem utterly unrelated concepts.

Could you please defend your *assertion* and then demonstrate how your
*conclusion* flows incontrovertibly from it?

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
Clark F. Morris, Jr. wrote:
> Chuck Stevens wrote:
>>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> (1956- 1957) let alone whether that would be currently accurate.

I think there's an historical background to this. I'll go from memory 
but I have one of those coffee table books with beautiful pictures of UK 
accompanied by a 'potted history'.

In the UK - certainly from the Norman Conquest on, (1066) the villeins 
attending their three strips of land, used in a tri-cycle of 
cultivation, had a life expectancy - females up to about 25 and male, if 
lucky, made it to 35. That coupled with a lousy basic diet of grains,
(still remembered today in the traditional Scottish porridge - simmered 
oat flakes in water), and no veggies. Hygiene was appalling and a very 
large mortality rate at births both infants and their mothers, coupled 
with the possibility of infants not living living beyond the baby years. 
So as a necessity, from very ancient times, it made sense for girls to 
enter into marriage as soon as they reached puberty.

Meanwhile to accommodate any Malthus predictions - the Plague and the 
Black Death.

Same book puts numbers on the Roman Invasion, not Caesar, but his 
successor Claudius. They fielded 20,000 legionnaires - that's one hell 
of a number. My guess Britain (England, Wales and Scotland), possibly no 
more than 200,000 inhabitants - so using 20,000 soldiers was cheesecake.
As of the Elizabethan Age there were only some 4 million inhabitants. 
Not much growth if you take it from the Dawn of Time up to 1601.

Compare that to Cowtown Calgary - about 30,000 in 1940 and just starting 
to nudge 1 million to-day.

Jimmy

Jimmy, Calgary
```

_(thread depth: 45)_

- **From:** Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T10:37:34-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<joe_zitzelberger-D93A48.10373201052005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`
- **References:** `<1e50e.2183556$Zm5.353953@news.easynews.com> <405b1$42685021$45491db9$26469@KNOLOGY.NET> <d4afo8$n8j$1@panix5.panix.com> <c3174$426d92e7$45491c57$29869@KNOLOGY.NET> <d4k6a8$lvo$1@panix5.panix.com> <9ce33$426da3dc$45491c57$1040@KNOLOGY.NET> <d4lof0$10rm$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <2c83b$426ee3f2$45491c57$32370@KNOLOGY.NET> <d4omb9$2plj$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <d4osmg$df6$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <9e01d$42704bde$45491c57$12739@KNOLOGY.NET> <d4qqt5$qnu$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <J39ce.1147476$Xk.456305@pd7tw3no> <sNbce.10185$BW6.1017762@news20.bellglobal.com> <joe_zitzelberger-E80D96.22090030042005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <K1_ce.34727$5f.11651@tornado.tampabay.rr.com>`

```
In article <K1_ce.34727$5f.11651@tornado.tampabay.rr.com>,
 "jce" <defaultuser@hotmail.com> wrote:

> "Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message 
> news:joe_zitzelberger-E80D96.22090030042005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> JCE 

I suppose I could re-title the thread "Evolution or Creation of 
Programming Language Names".  Placing myself solidly in the Evolutionist 
camp.

Just as "Radar", "Laser" and "Scuba" have become words by merit of being 
pronounceable acronyms, so "C-O-B-O-L" has become "Cobol".  It is the 
natural order of things.  Now that Merriam-Webster dictionary officially 
recognizes "Cobol" as a valid variant I feel no need to change.

Furthermore, there is solid evidence from the historical record of 
programming language names that they do evolve and mutate over time once 
released into the wild.  Consider, for example:

On the first day N. Wirth created the pure and perfect programming 
language.  On the second day he observed it and decided it was good.  So 
on the third day he named it "Pascal" after the mathematician.  So on 
the fourth day he released it into the wild.  

Pascal began to creep up everywhere -- but it also began to change in 
unexpected ways.  Some variants grew a "VS/" appendage and began 
operating in uppercase.  While others adapted thread libraries for their 
own needs.  Others adapted a byte-code interpreter to allow them to 
operate in almost any environment.  Finally, one mutation overtook all 
of the others, it had a "Turbo" appendage attached to its name.

Other Pascal variants were simply unable to compete.  The "Turbo Pascal" 
variant gobbled up market share and began to consume the habitat of all 
the other Pascal offshoots. 

You might think the process stopped there, but it did not, once "Turbo 
Pascal" became dominant it continued to mutate.  Some of the mutations 
were wild and unexpected -- as different as horses and zebras -- these 
were often grouped into their own families like Eiffel, Oberon and 
Modula.  But still other variants continued in the Pascal family.  
Pascal types that had evolved the "Object" extension to their name were 
proving themselves better able to adapt and began to displace the other 
types.

Once again, a dominant mutation arose from the "Object Pascal" -- its 
name was as wildly different from the original as the language was.  It 
called itself "Delphi".

Even though it became dominant among the Pascal family, it was hard 
pressed to fight off encroachment by the "visual" families and the weird 
beast called "java".  Finally, habitat destruction on the windows 
platform forced it to evolve yet again into a variant that could survive 
in the Linux environment -- this time it called itself "Klix".

I mention all of this to point out thatCOBOLs creators might well have 
intended it to always be called COBOL.  But they were blinded by the 
adversary -- they themselves could not visualize lower-case.  Once they 
released Cobol into the wild it has changed.  Perhaps not as much as 
Pascal -- after all, most cobol is in a controlled environment, almost a 
preserve where competition is limited and the rigors of life are simple,  
any variant of Cobol that establishes itself is likely to survive for at 
least four decades.  Still, it has changed.  Some mutations have 
occurred, one of them is "Cobol".
```

```
I didn't mean to suggest that anyone should start a project to find and

   "if it isn't broken don't fix it"

It is more a case of something to avoid in new programs, or to consider
changing in a program which fails often, or which is being opened up for
major revision.

I find having stand alone sort utilities on IBM mainframes very convenient.

When I worked on Honeywell GCOS applications in the late 1970s the only
simple way to do a sort was to invoke it from a COBOL-74 program. If I recall
correctly the only examples in their sort utility were invoking it from
COBOL-74 and from GMACS assembler. Ironically we were never able to get
Honeywell PL/I programs to compile, even though their COBOL-74 compiler was 
apparently written in their PL/I and would do PUT SKIPs when it aborted.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ SORT - Internal vs External (was: Performance problem with a VSAM files

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T17:10:12+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<U38de.1663026$za2.263680@news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<2yhbe.9244$oy2.391259@weber.videotron.net> <d4kthk$2q8$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <EaCbe.903$iP.281556@weber.videotron.net> <d4nhqf$j3i$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <tT4de.646$SI2.45485@wagner.videotron.net> <d530ra$fb8$1@theodyn.ncf.ca>`

```
This is aimed and IBM z/OS shops.  There may or may not be "comparable" 
discussions for other COBOL environments.

The issue of "internal" (COBOL) vs "external" (JCL/utility) SORTs has been going 
on as long as PARAGRAPH vs SECTION discussions - and really comes down to the 
same issues:

 - What is YOUR shop Standard
 - What will programmers be familiar with (for maintenance and for 3 a.m. calls)
 - Style, STYLE, style

If your shop uses a currently supported  IBM COBOL compiler and uses the FASTSRT 
compiler option, there is NEVER any significant difference between job stream 
with
  - program-logic program
  - external sort
 - program logic program

      versus

Internal program with SORT having Input Procedure and Output Procedure

     Versus

SORT with E15 and E35 exits in COBOL

When there is no logic on either the input or the output side, the two also 
perform comparably.

   ***

Certain types of application design can make one better than the other.

If your site needs the SORTED output file for multiple other applications, you 
CAN use an internal SORT for one and then catalog the output in an OUTPUT 
procedure, but I (personally) would find that type of system "difficult to 
maintain".

If your "input" is something like an IMS database or something that SORT doesn't 
handle "easily" you CAN create a "temporary" file and then have an EXTERNAL sort 
handle it and then process the sorted records, but I (personally) find that type 
of application error-prone as something changes on the input side but the output 
program doesn't get modified).

Bottom-Line:
   Yes we can have another "useless" and long-winded thread on why "I" (whoever 
is posting) like one or the other,  but I do not (personally <G>) like people 
who try and tell other shops (or programmers) to
    NEVER use an internal SORT
          or
    ALWAYS use an internal SORT

P.S.  And although internal sort programs are MORE portable to other compilers 
and operating systems, this is another long outstanding question of how many 
z/OS COBOL programs actually DO ever get ported to other environments - 
certainly some, but is this a critical "new program" design consideration or 
not.  I have mixed feelings.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: SORT - Internal vs External (was: Performance problem with a VSAM files

```

"William M. Klein" (wmklein@nospam.netcom.com) writes:
> This is aimed and IBM z/OS shops.  There may or may not be "comparable" 
> discussions for other COBOL environments.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> same issues:

At the extreme I've seen what should be mutiple step jobs coded as a single
program with several imbedded sorts. If anything in the chain failed it was
fix the problem and start from the beginning one more time, with feeling.

Program memory reduces the amount available for sorting. That varies with
pogram size. Exits are usually realtively small. In these days of XA, ESA
and even 64 bit adressing that isn't as big an issue as it once was, but
it can sometimes be deadly.

I once got burned by Syncsort deciding to do a gigabyte internal sort for
IMS Prefix resolution with no XA memory and no hiperspace. Apparently it
tried to be a good MVS citizen by adjusting it's use of XA and hiperspace
memory depending on the load. The proprietary algorithm sometimes got
that wrong, failing to use high memory or hiperspaces when there was a low
demand for memory in the system.

A Prefix Resolution step which took 20 minutes in a full size trial run
abended out of space after 8 hours. This occurred with about 90% consistency
on saturday day shift and about 2% consistency on weeknights. The vendor
eventually provided a fix after I provided SPYSET and BMSG output.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Performance problem with a VSAM files

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca>
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T21:17:18-03:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3dncecF6qrd4pU1@uni-berlin.de>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<tT4de.646$SI2.45485@wagner.videotron.net>`
- **References:** `<2yhbe.9244$oy2.391259@weber.videotron.net> <d4kthk$2q8$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <EaCbe.903$iP.281556@weber.videotron.net> <d4nhqf$j3i$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <tT4de.646$SI2.45485@wagner.videotron.net>`

```
JRDuval wrote:

> "Kelly Bert Manning" <bo774@FreeNet.Carleton.CA> a ï¿½crit dans le message de
> news:d4nhqf$j3i$1@theodyn.ncf.ca...
…[53 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Internal sorts are fine.  As someone else pointed out, on modern 
hardware for most circumstances, memory is not a problem (unless your 
application is running 24 bit for some peculiar reason). Internal sorts 
at least have to go through the installation's change control for 
programs and on the IBM z series are clearer than most of the utility 
sorts.  SORT FIELDS=(12,5,PD,A,24,32,CH,A) doesn't really tell me what 
fields are being sorted.  If you have SYNCSORT on HP-UX and use the 
COBOL copybook option for the record description, that is a different 
story.

In regard to the problem of the load, the best way is to describe the 
file as ORGANIZATION INDEXED ACCESS SEQUENTIAL and open it OUTPUT.  As 
someone else posted the DD statement (I am assuming MVS, OS390 or z/OS) 
should have an AMP parameter with BUFND=2*(data-CIs-per-track+1) and 
BUFNI=(1+index-set) where the index set is the number of index CIs 
needed to address each of the control areas.  If you have 8192 byte data 
CIs you get 6 per 3390 track.  Do not have a data CI (control interval 
equivalent of a block) less than 4096 bytes.  Make certain the size of 
your index CIs is large enough to contain the keys for all of the data 
CIs in the control area (1 cylinder) and the larger the key size, the 
more attention must be paid to the CI size since keys may not compress 
as well as the IBM assumption says they will.  Also watch the number of 
index levels.  Allocate in cylinders or make certain the secondary 
quantity for number of records will cause a cylinder to be allocated. 
Use the AMP parameter on all DD statements for the VSAM files with the 
following exceptions: 1) Files that are input to or output from SYNCSORT 
which left to its own devices will allocate an optimal amount (the same 
may be true of the other SORT products), 2) Files that are defined to 
Systems managed storage for system determined buffering or otherwise 
fully controlled by a storage management product and 3) possibly files 
that are in USING and GIVING statements of the SORT verb since they may 
be taking advantage of the SORT optimizations.

I realize that the above is arcanity that must boggle the minds of those 
using non z series platforms and I believe that the system should be 
handling much of this.  However, with tuning like this a system designed 
in world of vastly different constraints can perform quite well in 
today's world.

Also those on other platforms note that the INDEXED file structure - 
VSAM on the IBM z series (and predecessors) is an operating system 
component for which COBOL only uses a subset of the facilities 
available.  VSAM is accessible by most of the report programs (DYL280 
now Vision something or another, Easytrieve, etc.) and by appropriate 
system utilities.  PL/1 definitely has VSAM capability, I assume that 
C/C++ has extensions for VSAM and Fortran may know what to do with VSAM. 
  Indeed one of the interesting decisions when going to a Unix or 
Windows platform is whether to convert all of the VSAM to the relational 
database being used or get the add on INDEXED file system for the 
platform.  The conversion to the relational database is the better long 
term strategy and probably saves software license cost but getting the 
add-on INDEXED file system will cut the amount of change.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Performance problem with a VSAM files

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** bo774@FreeNet.Carleton.CA (Kelly Bert Manning)
- **Date:** 2005-05-03T15:22:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d584vc$hdg$1@theodyn.ncf.ca>`
- **References:** `<2yhbe.9244$oy2.391259@weber.videotron.net> <d4kthk$2q8$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <EaCbe.903$iP.281556@weber.videotron.net> <d4nhqf$j3i$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <tT4de.646$SI2.45485@wagner.videotron.net> <3dncecF6qrd4pU1@uni-berlin.de>`

```

"Clark F. Morris, Jr." (cfmtech@istar.ca) writes:

>> 
> Internal sorts are fine.  As someone else pointed out, on modern 
> hardware for most circumstances, memory is not a problem (unless your 
> application is running 24 bit for some peculiar reason). Internal sorts 

Or the sort product itself, as I mentioned earlier. Deciding not to use
any XA memory or hiperspace for Gbyte sorts is not an ideal way for a sort
product to establish a reputation for consistent, reliable, performance.
If I recall correctly the BMSG output showed Syncsort was trying to sort
1.5 Gbytes with less than 250K of 24 bit memory and nothing else. That was
all that IMS Prefix Resolution left for it. Going back through our late
completion reports for a while and checking step EXCP reports on the late
jobs I noticed that Syncsort was occassionaly doing this for standalone sorts,
they just didn't slow down quite as much because they had more 24 bit
memory to work with.

I'm a fan of Keep it Simple. Simple processes are usually easier to debug
and get working reliably. If they are handling huge volumes of data then
passing records directly to the sort or receiving them directly from the
sort can be a way of speeding things up, but for small time sorts it makes
things more more complex to restart if they fail.

A program with an imbedded sort is at least 2 separate processes, sometimes
more. I've  seen them go even farther, concatenating several different
processing phases into a single program with sorts or merges between the
phases.

When you are processing hundreds of millions of records taking up gigabytes
of space that may be worth the extra complexity, but for smaller processes
it probably isn't worth it. 

If they need to be combined to reduce I/O and elasped time then that 
should be done, but look at the numbers first, consider the failure modes
and rerun and restart processing if it fails.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Performance problem with a VSAM files

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2005-05-03T18:03:21+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<J1Pde.17709$GG5.11068@fe01!news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<2yhbe.9244$oy2.391259@weber.videotron.net> <d4kthk$2q8$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <EaCbe.903$iP.281556@weber.videotron.net> <d4nhqf$j3i$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <tT4de.646$SI2.45485@wagner.videotron.net> <3dncecF6qrd4pU1@uni-berlin.de> <d584vc$hdg$1@theodyn.ncf.ca>`

```
I am more familiar with DFSort than SyncSort - but how recent was your "bad" 
SyncSort internal sort problem?  Were you using the FASTSRT compiler option?

I would CERTAINLY not recommend using an old compiler (pre-FASTSRT) or an old 
release of any SORT product.

Most (not all) of the "internal sort" problems that I have heard of are "well, 
we had this problem 10 years ago, so we haven't used it since".

As I indicated in another reply, there certainly ARE cases where an external 
sort better "fits" an application design - but the same can be said (IMHO) with 
internal sorts.  If you have a "3 step job - that simply creates a temporary 
dataset, SORTs it, and then process the output of a SORT - then, I think, this 
belongs as in internal SORT.
```

#### ↳ Re: Performance problem with a VSAM files

- **From:** Andreas Lerch <andreas@andreas-lerch.de>

```


>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>> Ursprüngliche Nachricht <<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<

Am 26.04.05, 02:17:13, schrieb "JRDuval" <jrduval@videotron.ca> zum 
Thema Performance problem with a VSAM files:


> Hi.

> Since last week we're experiencing  performance problem when writing 
to a
> VSAM file.
> Context:
> We have a big master compressed VSAM file (about 30 million records) 
that we
> read to decompress it and writing it into 50 sequentiel files 
depending on
> wich record we read.  For one of them we decided to change it into a 
VSAM
> file for convenience. This is the biggest one with more than 1 million
> records.

> Problem:
> For some reason, when finishing testing we were amazed to see that it 
took
> 10 times more CPU time to run that our other program and that much 
I/O. The
> only thing that I could thing of was to put back our older program and 
run
> an IDCAMS to convert it into a VSAM file. Is there a problem with 
Cobol when
> writing big VSAM file or is it just some kind of limitation when using 
VSAM
> files into Cobol program.

> Is there another way to do it instead of using  IDCAMS.

> Thank you for your input and good night..

Hello

in MVS there ist a subsystem named BLSR (Batch Local Shared Resources) 
which can handle VSAM-reads better than every application programm - 
it can be activated on JCL only, its a simple change.

The other is the evaluation of the output files, can you determine it 
binary. Thats better than do a average of 25 questions to fill 50 
files.
Evaluate True
when file equal 01 write file01
when file equal 02 write file02
when file equal 11 write file11
when file equal 21 write file21

..... and so on can be raplaced by

Evaluate True
when filebyte1 equal 0
evaluate true
when filebyte2 equal 1 write file01
when filebyte2 equal 2 write file02
evaluate true
when filebyte1 equal 1
evaluate true
when filebyte2 equal 1 write file11
..... and so on

this are 2,5 + 5 avarage questions ist the filenumber is from 0 to 49

One other is the sequence READ INTO, WRITE FROM. If you can do this: 
READ FD-File - WRITE FROM input-definition, you will save one move.

READ INTO --> takes the next buffer-offset and makes a move into the 
storage-area
WRITE FROM --> takes the next buffer-address and makes a move from 
storage to the buffer

maybe, there are a lot of solutions!

Einen schoenen Tag
Andreas Lerch
```

## Thread: How to make IBM COBOL send in text in Telnet mode? — 12 this month / 15 total

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: How to make IBM COBOL send in text in Telnet mode?

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T14:46:41+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d52q4h01mdf@news4.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<1114055159.725184.76020@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <d4b98o02o7l@news3.newsguy.com> <1114789034.177346.280990@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`

```

In article <1114789034.177346.280990@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>, dave@pixieware.com writes:
> 

Please don't top-post.  Intersperse new text in your reply with concise
quoted material relevant to it:

   A: Because it messes up the order in which people normally read text.
   Q: Why is top-posting such a bad thing?
   A: Top-posting.
   Q: What is the most annoying thing on usenet and in e-mail?
      -- David Bonde

[I've also reformatted wrapped lines in the following quoted material.]

> Our method does rely on a IIS extension, so IIS is really acting as a
> Telnet client. What I need is for AIX to act as a Telnet server, and
> I need a COBOL program to send/receive to/from the Telnet server.

AIX has a Telnet server, like every Unix implementation for the past
20 years or so.  AIX itself does not "act as a Telnet server" -
either something is a Telnet server or it isn't, and with AIX as with
most OSes it's implemented separately from the kernel.  I want to be
precise here because with distributed applications it's vital to have
an accurate understanding of how they work.

In the case of AIX (at least with 4.2; I haven't bothered checking a
more recent version), the telnet server is /usr/sbin/telnetd, and it's
usually run under the auspices of inetd.  This is typical for Unix
systems.

Running a COBOL program under a Telnet server is easy.  Normally you'd
just log in through Telnet and then execute it under your login shell.
That's the whole point of Telnet, after all.

If you want to cut out the middleman - the shell - then just do so.
telnetd runs /usr/sbin/login, which requests a username and (optionally)
a password and then runs whatever's listed as the initial program for
that username in /etc/passwd.  Normally that's a shell, but it could
be your COBOL program.  (Note that you need superuser permission to
assign a program not listed in /etc/shells as the initial program for
an account.)

For example, you create an entry in /etc/passwd that looks like this:

   myapp::500:100:COBOL application:/home/myapp:/path/to/myapp

(adjusting all of those fields as appropriate for your installation)
and have your Telnet client log in as "myapp".  (In this case, no
password is required, because the second field above is empty.)
login will run /path/to/myapp automatically, and its console I/O will
be through the pty which is attached to the Telnet connection, just
as if it were a shell.

That would still require that your Telnet client - the IIS extension -
pass a username to login when prompted for it.  If it's not capable of
doing that, I think you'll have to find another Telnet server (there
are open-source ones available), as I don't believe AIX telnetd has an
option to invoke anything other than /usr/sbin/login once Telnet nego-
tiation has finished.

The best references for implementing Telnet in Unix are W. Richard
Stevens' classic _Advanced Programming in the UNIX Environment_ and
_UNIX Network Programming_, but you shouldn't need that level of detail.
A book on Unix system administration should explain the relationships
among telnetd, login, and the shell or other initial program in more
detail if this is unclear.

> I will look at the pseudo-tty interface.

Unless you're writing your own Telnet server you don't need to.

Now, things will be trickier on the AS/400, because OS/400 and
applications written for it are designed to be used with block-mode
5250 terminals, and with the TN5250 Telnet variant which emulates
them.  OS/400 does have support for "classic" Telnet for terminal
types that it recognizes such as VT100 (they have to provide adequate
support for things like cursor positioning so that it can emulate
full-screen mode by drawing each screenful of data).  Since COBOL
applications on OS/400 typically want to do block-mode screen-by-
screen displays, it may be difficult to get streaming console I/O
from a COBOL program.  The various OS/400 C implementations have a
special stream-I/O mode, to support the vast code base of C programs
that expect to do console I/O that way, but I'm not sure it's
available to COBOL programs.

Out of curiousity I just tried a little COBOL program on OS/400 that
did a couple of displays, and it displayed a screen with a "job
started" line and the first line of output; I had to hit Enter to get
the next line, and it looked like it was positioning the cursor for
it.  Not simply streaming console output, in other words.

I'd suggest taking up the AS/400 issues on comp.sys.ibm.as400.misc.
If there's a FAQ for that group, it may already be addressed there.

Personally, a Telnet connection would not be my choice for this
application anyway.  Telnet is not designed for tunnelling applica-
tion to application data.  (The RFCs mention that possibility, and
NVT is used for some connections that are typically app-to-app like
FTP and SMTP, but it was a secondary consideration.)  There's a
reason why HTTP was invented: to transmit HTML and related data.  It'd
be much cleaner to just run your COBOL applications as CGI programs
under an HTTP server.
```

<a id="t-d2de2bbff5"></a>
## Thread: IBM Help — 2 this month / 5 total

_Thread started [2005-04](2005-04.md#t-d2de2bbff5)._

#### ↳ Re: IBM Help

- **From:** mwojcik@newsguy.com (Michael Wojcik)
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T16:40:45+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d530qd0qor@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<Auwce.141$8g.64@news01.roc.ny>`

```

In article <Auwce.141$8g.64@news01.roc.ny>, Floyd Johnson <floydj@netins.net> writes:
> I have student who has received a very attractive internship offer (i.e. 
> room and board, transportation, and a small salary) because of the 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> However, the internship includes work with TSO, IPSF (?), ReXX, etc. 

It's ISPF - a character-based full-screen editor and IDE.  ISPF is
one of what Bezroukov calls the "Eastern Orthodox editors": a
family of source-code editors (mostly derived from the unrelated but
similar ISPF for TSO and XEDIT for CMS) which share significant look
and feel.  (Bezroukov's terminology and opinions are idiosyncratic,
but his site[1] does give something of a feel for the differences
between the IBM mainframe editors and the major Unix editors.)

It's been quite some time since I did anything significant with ISPF,
though I have used OS/400's SEU, another (lesser) member of the
family, recently.  So take the rest of this with a grain of salt.

Some of the notable features of ISPF and relations:

- They're designed for block-mode terminals.  Consequently, while
they support full-screen editing, changes to lines on the screen
actually take effect when the user presses an action key, like Enter
or a page-scrolling key.

- They have a command line for more complex editing tasks.

- They have a left margin area on the screen with line numbers.  The
line numbers can be overtyped with commands such as "I" to insert
lines below the overtyped line.

- They can "fold" the source view, hiding ranges of lines so that,
for example, you might have only section headers on the screen at one
point; then you could unfold one section to view its contents.

The only free editor of this type I know of is THE, The Hessling
Editor, which is based on XEDIT.[2]  It'd give your student a feel
for the ISPF UI, though.

There are commercial ISPF ports to other platforms (back around 1985
I had one for PC DOS, which I used for several years), so there might
be a free one out there which I haven't run into.


TSO (Time Sharing Option) is an add-on for MVS (Multiple Virtual
Storage), one of the major IBM mainframe OSes.  MVS isn't designed
for interactive use; TSO provides that.  Typically TSO is used to
run ISPF so you can do program development and that sort of thing
on MVS systems.


IBM's manuals for ISPF and TSO are available from IBM's website
somewhere.  There might be online tutorials, too.


Someone else has already noted that there are numerous free Rexx
implementations, tutorials, etc available.  THE also supports Rexx
macros.


I'm a Unix programmer by preference, and Unix and Windows are where I
do most of my work, but I quite enjoy switching gears once in a while
and doing a little mainframe (or even OS/400) development.  Some
people find changing their work habits difficult and annoying - which
I understand - but I hope your student finds the mainframe environ-
ment palatable.  Even if his career takes him in other directions, I
think it will be a useful experience.


1. http://www.softpanorama.org/Editors/index.shtml
2. http://sourceforge.net/projects/hessling-editor
```

## Thread: Net Express 4 — 4 msgs

### Net Express 4

- **From:** mark <meandme1963@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T18:59:09+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d531st$2g$1@ctb-nnrp2.saix.net>`

```
Hi

I am newbie to MF Cobol Net Express 4. How can i get Net Express 4 to 
allow the reserved word SCREEN as not being a reserved word. Also allow 
it to accept variables ending with a hyphen. (Some switch/directive ?)

If this can be controled with directives, can i place them in my project 
options or where would be the best to place them.? I have about 250 
programs migrating from old vscobol unix.

please help

Thanks
Mark
```

#### ↳ Re: Net Express 4

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T17:39:55+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Lv8de.1184181$Xk.269726@pd7tw3no>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<d531st$2g$1@ctb-nnrp2.saix.net>`
- **References:** `<d531st$2g$1@ctb-nnrp2.saix.net>`

```
mark wrote:
> Hi
> 
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Not sure, but without searching I'm inclined to think SCREEN is cast in 
stone as a RESERVED word. There may possibly be directives setting your 
compiler to older COBOL versions which exclude some currently RESERVED 
words, but then you might have problems where they exclude RESERVED 
words that you want !

Bear in mind that SCREEN or any other RESERVED word is no longer 
reserved if you tag something on the front or end, e.g. :-

ws-SCREEN or SCREEN-1

Your problem you've got some 250 programs to check out, but it's 
probably only a percentage of those that actually contain the word SCREEN ?

As a thought, try this using the N/E IDE :-

Main Menu Bar ----> Search---->Find in Files - then enter the word 
Screen or SCREEN

The result will bring up an output list with EVERY program in your 
current application containing the word SCREEN. Make a note of the 
program names.

Open each relevant program as your source. Back to the Search - from the 
Menu Bar---->Search-----Find Text (i.e. in the current source)

Brings up the Find/Replace box - you can now do a global replace :-

Make 'Screen' into 'A-Screen'.

It might appear half-baked, but that's the way I would go at it. And 
REBUILD ALL is going to highlight any you miss.

Your variables ending with an hyphen - I very much doubt that. Check the 
on-line or the N/E on-line Language Reference Manual. Again if that's a 
big problem, try repeating what I've written above about 'correcting' 
SCREEN.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

#### ↳ Re: Net Express 4

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T11:54:52-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1114973692.503326.254500@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<d531st$2g$1@ctb-nnrp2.saix.net>`
- **References:** `<d531st$2g$1@ctb-nnrp2.saix.net>`

```
> SCREEN as not being a reserved word.

NOMF

See the MF Compiler directive and there should be a huge table
somewhere in the docs (language ref appendix probably) that lists all
reserved words and which directives change them (if any).

REMOVE"SCREEN"

will also remove just the one reserved word.

> Also allow it to accept variables ending with a hyphen

See Language ref, chapter: Concepts of the Cobol Language, section:
User-defined words:

"except that '-' can not appear as first or last character".

This is part of the Cobol standard.  The solution is probably to
process the source code and change these.
```

#### ↳ Re: Net Express 4

- **From:** "Kellie Fitton" <KELLIEFITTON@YAHOO.COM>
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T12:05:00-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1114974300.488825.295270@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<d531st$2g$1@ctb-nnrp2.saix.net>`
- **References:** `<d531st$2g$1@ctb-nnrp2.saix.net>`

```
Mark,

Net Express compiler has a compiler directive called
REMOVE that you can use to isolate a reserveWord
into a user-defined words under windows 2000 pro,
you should try it under unix and see if you can use it
with your OS environment, you can code it as follows
on the FIRST line of your source code:

$set REMOVE "reserve-word"

Hope these information helps.

Kellie.
```

## Thread: Micro Focus Cobol / 2 Workbench For Sale — 1 this month / 7 total

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

_Thread started [2005-04](2005-04.md#t-9d56f867a3)._

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Micro Focus Cobol / 2 Workbench For Sale

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** bo774@FreeNet.Carleton.CA (Kelly Bert Manning)
- **Date:** 2005-05-01T17:43:02+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d534f6$he5$1@theodyn.ncf.ca>`
- **References:** `<ScSbe.5096$wu2.171@newsfe1-gui.ntli.net> <oqSbe.1144519$8l.1011694@pd7tw1no> <d4p0le$erv$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <d4qpa3$4j5$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <d4r104$jcc$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <ZI8ce.5796733$Zm5.888187@news.easynews.com> <d4rfhl$r7s$1@theodyn.ncf.ca> <uWbce.1363300$za2.217063@news.easynews.com>`

```

"William M. Klein" (wmklein@nospam.netcom.com) writes:

> I would have hoped that you could have found the appropriate people to work 
> with - within Micro Focus (or within IBM for LE and PL/I) to get TIMELY 
> assistance - but it sure sounds like that did NOT happen.

Actually we did get quite a high level of support from IBM for LE migration.
The issue seemed to be that the risk of application error handler exits not
getting it correct weren't understood. 

At one point one of the LE developers showed up for a site visit. He and I
were the only men there wearing sandals. In my case that was because I had
come in on short notice for the meeting from a "vacation" day I'd booked to
paint the exterior of my house and only had time to take off my coverall
and slip into sandals before heading to the meeting. Ms. Brown, his manager,
told us that he was actually looking particularly presentable that day,
having touched base with his wife to get him tidied up. She showed up for
site visits several other times as well.

I mentioned to the developer an example of an Application PL/I ON ERROR
block which began with PUT DATA; instead of ON ERROR SYSTEM;

When invoked by a data exception it looped until cancelled.

Most of the issues seem to be a misconception about error handling in
environments such as IMS, CICS and DB2.

The promoters of LE made a big PR point of saying that something to the
effect that 
   now in the event of an error you can get control, fully resolve it
   and continue processing

Perhaps some people are clever enough to code a one size fits all error
handling routine that is 100% guaranteed to handle all possible errors,
without exception. I know I'm not that smart. 

For example, failing to enable language features to check for references
beyond the bounds of an array or string can corrupt executable code. At
that point all bets are off. The program may also have corrupted values
needed to issue an IMS rollback call, for example, after a data exception
caused by these types of errors.

The LE refusal to honour NOSTAE,NOSPIE in PLXOPT external variables, for
example, actually made it incompatible with the IMS requirement that
application code never get control in such cases.
 
That is, TRAP(ON) is similar to running STAE, but the IMS manuals said
that PL/I had to run NOSTAE to ensure that IMS could backout to the last
point of consistency if an error occurred. Getting everything coordinated
took a while.

The issue was that if application code gets control and doesn't resolve
problems correctly you can end up with an IMS DB or DB2 table which is
not consistent with your business rules. 

My advice is to have IMS or DB2 programs abend with errors in application
code, rather than having application code try to fix things up.

http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/ceeu1007/5.4.8?DT=19950913203351

It took a while to get the concept across and for IBM to provide a way to
disable application error handlers in production application code while 
allowing normal operating system or DBMS abend or interrupt action outside
application code.

It is sometimes useful for application error handlers to get control in
testing or debugging situations but they are dangerous for production
IMS or DB2 applications.
```
